  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8c5c3a86-c429-4fbb-b375-e5e9ec3f0751'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1636645525902811015">Roman čija se radnja zbiva u zagrebačkoj sredini uoči Drugog svjetskog rata i na njegovu početku, a u središtu autorova interesa su krugovi intelektualaca, poluintelektualaca i umjetnika, izbezumljenost i izgubljenost čitave jedne generacije. Glavni lik, Melkior, novinski je kritičar koji proživljava mučne dane očekujući poziv u vojsku i trapeći se pritom teškim postom, ne</span>
  <span id="freeText1636645525902811015" style="display:none">Roman čija se radnja zbiva u zagrebačkoj sredini uoči Drugog svjetskog rata i na njegovu početku, a u središtu autorova interesa su krugovi intelektualaca, poluintelektualaca i umjetnika, izbezumljenost i izgubljenost čitave jedne generacije. Glavni lik, Melkior, novinski je kritičar koji proživljava mučne dane očekujući poziv u vojsku i trapeći se pritom teškim postom, ne bi li tako, postigavši iscrpljenost i asteniju, izbjegao regrutaciju. Kreće se središtem Zagreba, posjećujući svoju redakciju, određene kavane i ljubavnicu Enku, te boraveći u podstanarskoj sobici. Sa svojim - uglavnom novinarskim - društvom, on provodi noći u pijanim i ciničkim raspravama. Zaljubljen je u izvjesnu Vivijanu, koja ima mnoge ljubavnike, ali njega zaobilazi. Duh mu je zaokupljen pitanjima o krivnji, budućnosti i ljudožderstvu. Ubrzo nakon što je ipak regrutiran, dospijeva u bolnicu, a potom u ludnicu, ali uskoro biva pušten i iz vojske, i iz ludnice. Nakon izlaska ophrva ga još veća praznina i strah, a njegova duševna i duhovna dezintegriranost kulminira kad rat već bjesni, a njegov prijatelj Maestro počini samoubojstvo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1636645525902811015" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/785313514?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780300152418" href="/book/show/7968762-cyclops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2327886-jonfaith?shelf=balkan">balkan</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer785313514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13747891151415719483"><b>The historical tomorrow: and when men discovered the divine power of matter, it came to reign over them, confusing their minds, blighting their lives and then swallowing itself and turning into a Force which destroyed all laws and there is now not a single consciousness left that could proclaim it stupid in the name of Hegel.</b><br/><br/>Ranko Marinkovic's Cyclops is likely my favorite novel of the year. It is a Ulysses of Zagreb in 1940 with the howl of War in the air. A theatre critic fears for the future </span>
  <span id="freeText13747891151415719483" style="display:none"><b>The historical tomorrow: and when men discovered the divine power of matter, it came to reign over them, confusing their minds, blighting their lives and then swallowing itself and turning into a Force which destroyed all laws and there is now not a single consciousness left that could proclaim it stupid in the name of Hegel.</b><br /><br />Ranko Marinkovic's Cyclops is likely my favorite novel of the year. It is a Ulysses of Zagreb in 1940 with the howl of War in the air. A theatre critic fears for the future and pines for a certain beauty, he ignores her particulars and ascribes her a mythic persona. The critic worries about conscription and notes how his weight plummets. His paranoid caprice continues apace on a story about castaways imprisoned by cannibals.<br /><br />The Joycean delight takes a number of dark turns. The narrative rests in purgatory, a Catch-22 for the Damned. It returns to Zagreb as the Wehrmacht are poised to invade Yugoslavia. What unfolds is a bizarre variation on the Ithaca episode. <br /><br />The novel would've benefited from footnotes, especially towards the Serbo-Croat literary references. Despite my minor qualm, Cyclops is a staggering work <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13747891151415719483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer199642043" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13686978175728239163">i had a lot to say about this book when i finished it 3 weeks ago but then i didn't log onto this site and forgot what i was going to type because i'm fat stupid and short of breath. sort of reminded my of celine i guess, but also other stuff. anyway if you're interested in european modernism then "czech" this out. haha, he's really a croat tho.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anica" href="/user/show/13768588-anica">Anica</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8056967538801752537">When I was in 1st grade of high school, the older ones were saying to me that this book is so tough to read, and I always thought "Oh my, what's waiting for me?!"#$ Then came my turn to read this book. Honestly,I totally enjoyed since the first page. I used to read so many books about the life during the WW2 in some big cities like Paris, London, etc. but finally I found the first book which place and rime of action were situated in my hometown. I was able to feel all that melancholy and ZOO-pol</span>
  <span id="freeText8056967538801752537" style="display:none">When I was in 1st grade of high school, the older ones were saying to me that this book is so tough to read, and I always thought "Oh my, what's waiting for me?!"#$ Then came my turn to read this book. Honestly,I totally enjoyed since the first page. I used to read so many books about the life during the WW2 in some big cities like Paris, London, etc. but finally I found the first book which place and rime of action were situated in my hometown. I was able to feel all that melancholy and ZOO-polis term and idea on very very familiar places. An author brought all that prewar atmosphere and states of mind of very interesting characters so realistic that I found myself in one awkward moment of weird nostalgia. Which I never felt again in some other book with similar theme. I definitely recommend this book to anyone. It's not easy to read (like reading the book during Sun bathing on the beach), takes a lots of time to thinking, but if I have to summarize this in 1 word - masterpiece! </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_159564623" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159564623" />
    <a title="Monica Carter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2293616-monica-carter"><img alt="Monica Carter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330056990p2/2293616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/159564623?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monica Carter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monica Carter" href="/user/show/2293616-monica-carter">Monica Carter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780300152418" href="/book/show/7968762-cyclops">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2293616-monica-carter?shelf=btba">btba</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer159564623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12182240853923124667"> <blockquote>Reflected in the pale glass window, among the shoes on display, was Melkior's thin, unprepossessing silhouette, a poorly built city dweller. The slanting image reflected in the shop window triggered a crafty sneer inside Melkior, and the word mobilization suddenly found itself in autumn mud churned by a squelching olive drab monotony of dejected strangers on some endless trek; there was the bluster of angry sergeants, the tired voice of sodden boots, and the mysterious word "aide-de-camp." Here</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText12182240853923124667" style="display:none"> <blockquote>Reflected in the pale glass window, among the shoes on display, was Melkior's thin, unprepossessing silhouette, a poorly built city dweller. The slanting image reflected in the shop window triggered a crafty sneer inside Melkior, and the word mobilization suddenly found itself in autumn mud churned by a squelching olive drab monotony of dejected strangers on some endless trek; there was the bluster of angry sergeants, the tired voice of sodden boots, and the mysterious word "aide-de-camp." Here was born a fear of the new events around him: the driver bound for Apatin to drive a tank...across our mountainous country...Oh for a mountain and a forest in which to go quiet and still like an insect curled deep inside the bark of an indestructible tree: I'm not here...and to live, to live...How to conceal ones existence, steal from the world one's traitorous body, take it off to some endless isolation, conceal it in a cocoon of fear, insinuate oneself into a temporary death?</blockquote><br /><br />This is the test of our fair narrator-can Melkior starve himself to avoid being called to military duty in World War II? Filled with literary allusions galore-from Dante to Joyce, Shakespeare to Dosteyvesky-<i>Cyclops</i> is a vertiginous journey into the mind of a tortured man whose mind is unraveling from lack of food and sleep. Published in 1965 and set in the forties of the Former Yugoslavia, <i>Cyclops</i> is Marinkovic's version of literary realism so acute and ego-maniacal there is no escaping for the reader. The reader can only revel in his death defying acts of prose, no small thanks to Vlada Stojiljkovic's amazing translation. This is a classic taught in Croatian schools and with good reason-Marinkovic addresses the threat of human loss and sacrifice in the name of nationalism but also our own detachment from the cost of war when we are not directly involved.<br /><br />Melkior wanders the streets of Zagreb, lost in his own surreal musings dipped in paranoia. His own interactions with his gaggle of boho friends and his starving dream-states flow into one another until it becomes difficult to know what reality is. As he makes himself the star of the <i>Odyssey</i> and his friends replace Homer's other characters in his hallucinations, the danger of Polyphemus (The Cyclops) looms and threatens to devour them all. The parallels between society's own contradictions about war and Melkior's friends drinking themselves into a stupor(literally and figuratively) on their own intellectual antics shows itself throughout the fast-paced narrative. <br /><br />Above all, Melkior, a part-time theater critic, and his motley group of friends are poetic cynics who see no hope in anything but their own wittiness and pleasure seeking pastimes as they sit around downing booze at their local bar, the Give 'n Take.. Although this is not an easy book, it's wildly entertaining, full of original characterizations and hilarious, biting prose:<br /><br /> <blockquote>Long live the idiot! That is the safest kind of mimicry life can offer a being of its creation. From his vantage point the idiot watches history run its course without the danger of getting caught up in the action, just as we cry as we watch a film playing in the cinema. We mourn fictitious travails, while it's only an idiot who laughs at genuine deaths. He jeers at life from his safe vantage point, taking his revenge for being rejected, smug at being spared. Life has chosen Intelligence for its games, it does not use idiots to make history. It has chosen geniuses for grand words on the cross, at the guillotine, at the gallows, facing the barrels of guns, in front of nations cheering the Brutuses and Caesars alike. An idiot ceded the cup of poison to Socrates. An idiot ceded to Danton the glory of being decapitated by history. (And then made it up to him by producing a marble bust of his head and raising it on a square as an example for future generations.) Whereas the idiot wears his head with a strange grimace of disgust, as if he had long understood everything, sneered derisively, and stopped time in the rigid folds of his mindless face. Love live the idiot!</blockquote> <br /><br />There are so many literary allusions that you may wonder if you can possibly have read all the works he throws into his narrative stew. Yet, there is a conscious effort to avoid the reader getting close enough to experience Melkior's fear, instead choosing the have the reader bear witness to his descent into paranoia and isolation. <br /><br />It's an epic that hangs its hat on pessimism, social commentary, and the personal, societal and philosophical wreckage of war. I couldn't help but think of Celine's work while I read this- in particular, <i>Journey to the End of the Night</i> and <i>Normance</i>-both filled with the same disdain and realism of war. <i>Cyclops</i> doesn't give us the bravery and courage of the stereotyped patriot, but the very human fear of a man lost in his own life and fearful of a fate he knows he can't avoid. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12182240853923124667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2279624550" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2279624550" />
    <a title="Genndy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17770662-genndy"><img alt="Genndy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361709371p2/17770662.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2279624550?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Genndy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Genndy" href="/user/show/17770662-genndy">Genndy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2279624550" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3308478696196719483">This is supposed to be one of the most important Croatian modernist novels, and I can see why. It is so multi-layered and interwined that it is really a polyphonous cacophony of voices. Characters are very qoutable and psychology behind them is explored to the deapths. All characters are somewhat grotesque and degenerated, in one way or the other. At some point, you begin to question the sanity of the main character, but you are not really sure is he insane or are everyone around him insane, so </span>
  <span id="freeText3308478696196719483" style="display:none">This is supposed to be one of the most important Croatian modernist novels, and I can see why. It is so multi-layered and interwined that it is really a polyphonous cacophony of voices. Characters are very qoutable and psychology behind them is explored to the deapths. All characters are somewhat grotesque and degenerated, in one way or the other. At some point, you begin to question the sanity of the main character, but you are not really sure is he insane or are everyone around him insane, so he just looks insane. Therefore, you start questioning everything he says. Forthermore, dialogues feel so real, and yet they are so grotesque. Everyone who thinks this is just an anti-war novel needs to think again. This book is essentially anti-people, anti-society in general. Society is presented as a degenerated carnival madhouse, and that reading experience is sometimes hillarious and sometimes frightening.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3308478696196719483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155716267" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155716267" />
    <a title="Mark Van Aken Williams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4021718-mark-van-aken-williams"><img alt="Mark Van Aken Williams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279414465p2/4021718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155716267?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Van Aken Williams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Van Aken Williams" href="/user/show/4021718-mark-van-aken-williams">Mark Van Aken Williams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780300152418" href="/book/show/7968762-cyclops">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155716267" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12047414179888523838">Cyclops was originally published in Croatia. It takes place in Zagreb and could easily be compared to Ulysses, the Odyssey, and even Hamlet. Marinković captures the essence of the crowds, the shysters, and seedy neighborhoods. He dissects Zagreb on the eve of war with the same precision as Joseph Heller’s Catch 22. It is anti-war in sentiment— and was a prelude to similar books like Slaughter House Five, written in the 1960s with WWII as the setting. </span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

Just as good as I remember it, the best Croatian novel I'd ever read. By a long shot. Everything is brilliant. However, Croatian language is sort of silly at times; it's impossible to come up with complex sentences without sounding pretentious. Don't let that stop you from enjoying the book.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2134877761" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2134877761" />
    <a title="Joey Anderson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17432925-joey-anderson"><img alt="Joey Anderson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478026313p2/17432925.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2134877761?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey Anderson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey Anderson" href="/user/show/17432925-joey-anderson">Joey Anderson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11349826-kiklop">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2134877761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6743128978122616044">I highly recommend this overlooked novel of the eve and beginning of WWII in Zegreb, Croatia. The main character, Melkior, an everyman (and namesake of the oldest Magi) of sorts sees the war as if it is far away and in a distant land as do many of the characters of the novel. However, the thrust of Hitler’s military Germany is not far away as we discover in characters’ reading of newspapers about the London bombings in 1941. <br/><br/>Like another novel based on <i>The Odyssey (Ulysses)</i>, the situation and en</span>
  <span id="freeText6743128978122616044" style="display:none">I highly recommend this overlooked novel of the eve and beginning of WWII in Zegreb, Croatia. The main character, Melkior, an everyman (and namesake of the oldest Magi) of sorts sees the war as if it is far away and in a distant land as do many of the characters of the novel. However, the thrust of Hitler’s military Germany is not far away as we discover in characters’ reading of newspapers about the London bombings in 1941. <br /><br />Like another novel based on <i>The Odyssey (Ulysses)</i>, the situation and environs of the novel is quite narrow. The settings are a bar, the apartment house where Melkior lives, the apartment where his mistress lives, military barracks, an infirmary, a mental ward, and the streets of Zagreb. Melkior’s odyssey moves through these places as he attempts to avoid being drafted by starving himself and keeping his weight below the norm for military service.<br /><br />What the people desire in Zagreb is to be left alone as one character says that the Americans have it right by not desiring to get involved in European wars. Much of the action is dialogue in which the characters talk about theater, novels, and art (constant reference to Dostoevsky and Shakespeare; poor Yorick shows up frequently). A male-dominated novel, the men talk about women and their ever frustrating inability to complete their romantic dreams. Women, for the most part, are idealized figures except when the men encounter the real women of their dreams which they find are quite different from their dreams and quite difficult to control. If anything, the men in the novel hate that even though they fell superior to women, it is the women who control them and not vice-versa.<br /><br />To say the least, men are mocked in their quest for control of women and other men (competitors for women and power). The men lose out.<br /><br />In a real sense, Melkior’s inner life revolves around several female figures whom he loves or he believes should be in love with him. The only realistic figure is Enka, his mistress. <br /><br />The recurring allusion to Polyphemus, the Cyclops from Homer’s work, is a symbolic monster who desires to devour men and that is his singular obsession. While the main reference of the Cyclops is to Hitler, there are a variety of cyclopean figures who are bent on Melkior’s destruction.<br /><br />At least a third of the novel finds itself in Melkior’s thoughts where he composes a play about sailors who are shipwrecked on a South Seas island and find themselves in the hands of cannibals. Other mental landscapes illustrate Melkior’s dreams, both wishes and fears, as well as his constant daydreams.<br /><br />I found this novel to be much more American in how characters solve problems with their society and situation. Instead of trying to solve a problem, they desire to run, to escape. In America with its large geography, we can always run and if we find that America is not big enough, we can always head for the border of Mexico or Canada. <br /><br />Melkior seeks escape as does Huck Finn, Quentin Compson, the Joads, and others. While suicide is certainly an option that avails itself to some characters (Quentin Compson), Melkior desires territories to “lit out to” as Huck Finn desires or an idealized place like California that the Joads head for, but in Croatia and a German-dominated Europe, there is really no place to run to.<br /><br />I never found the novel boring or slow. Not only is it entertaining on a variety of levels, it is also thought provoking. Melkior is a great character, and we see Marindovic's great skill in keeping to his perspective in 550 pages. Not too many authors can do that. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6743128978122616044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3653211414" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3653211414" />
    <a title="Dirk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3132166-dirk"><img alt="Dirk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491174067p2/3132166.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3653211414?book_show_action=true">Nov 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dirk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dirk" href="/user/show/3132166-dirk">Dirk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780300152418" href="/book/show/7968762-cyclops">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3653211414" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12395361251014997958">In Cyclops, the main character, Melkior Tresic, attempts to avoid conscription in the run-up to World War II by starving himself into a condition in which he is not fit for service. He also hungers for nearly every beautiful woman who crosses his path, and, when they do not reciprocate his interest, denigrates them as beneath him. The point of view of this novel is generally a very close third person from Melkior's perspective in which the narrative flows in his river of consciousness, which is </span>
  <span id="freeText12395361251014997958" style="display:none">In Cyclops, the main character, Melkior Tresic, attempts to avoid conscription in the run-up to World War II by starving himself into a condition in which he is not fit for service. He also hungers for nearly every beautiful woman who crosses his path, and, when they do not reciprocate his interest, denigrates them as beneath him. The point of view of this novel is generally a very close third person from Melkior's perspective in which the narrative flows in his river of consciousness, which is fed from time to time from several different tributaries that spill into what might be thought of as the central action involving Melkior's neighbors, would-be friends, military authorities, and bar patrons and owners. As the synopsis indicates, the work is densely allusive, with many references, quotes, paraphrases, and echoes of Shakespeare, Greek mythology, poets of the former Yugoslavia, and others, so much so that one can debate whether Cyclops is a work of monumental genius or the author's literary pantheon of monuments to the geniuses that came before him.<br /><br />If, as F. Scott Fitzgerald said, action is character, Melkior's starved character is skeletal at best. He thinks, dreams, and speculates a lot, says very little, and does (or acts) even less. To me, he is like a squirrel that begins to run across a road, sees a car coming, hesitates, changes directions, hesitates again, and . . . A work of art can be enhanced or diminished by its frame, and in this instance it may be my frame of mind that keeps me from appreciating Cyclops, because in this age of social distancing I am doing so much dog paddling in circles in my own stream of consciousness that encountering a main character who lives in a wide-ranging but indecisive mind without materially engaging the world around him just doesn't do that much for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12395361251014997958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Melkior is not compelling, his 'gang' are idiots and his love (read: lust) interests are immature imbeciles.   <span id="freeText4911790468337794005" style="display:none">Simply awful. No allusion or reference to Odysseus, or Buddha, or Shakespeare, or anyone else for that matter, could save this hot mess. Melkior is not compelling, his 'gang' are idiots and his love (read: lust) interests are immature imbeciles. Even Atman, possibly the only character of interest, however slight, is misused (in my opinion). <br /><br />It could be the translation but nothing in this is fluid: the schizophrenic prose alternates between rudderless reality and meaningless fantasy, the characters are two dimensional at best, the 'draft' arc, although eliciting my compassion, was threadbare. There was never more than an ever-dwindling curiosity that kept me (speed) reading till the end. <br /><br />As for the life/death dichotomy element herein, some absurdists might find 'Cyclops' engaging, if not quite pleasurable.<br /><br />Bye bye Nobody!</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/8272524-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1342950870" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5604448648593542373">Since Ulysses every country has its comparative modern epic - its tale of Lonerhero wandering the streets of CountryCapital/CulturalCapital musing galactic penned with sylisticlinguistic mindfuckery. Kiklop (Cyclops) is Croatia's. I'm a dirty sucker for this kind of thing.<br/><br/>Marinkovic's Melkior - more Dedalus than Bloom - traipses around wartime (WW2) Zagreb hanging with the art crowd, falls in and out of lust with a manipulative hussy, does very little actual work, maintains a destructive relatio</span>
  <span id="freeText5604448648593542373" style="display:none">Since Ulysses every country has its comparative modern epic - its tale of Lonerhero wandering the streets of CountryCapital/CulturalCapital musing galactic penned with sylisticlinguistic mindfuckery. Kiklop (Cyclops) is Croatia's. I'm a dirty sucker for this kind of thing.<br /><br />Marinkovic's Melkior - more Dedalus than Bloom - traipses around wartime (WW2) Zagreb hanging with the art crowd, falls in and out of lust with a manipulative hussy, does very little actual work, maintains a destructive relationship with a married woman, escapes the war through mostly feigned mental illness, starves himself and witnesses the old being violently replaced by the new.<br /><br />Marinkovic's prose is more grounded than Joyce's madcappery though the pages are almost as thick with symbolism. Celine's ...s appear in regular use. There's also a whole lot of Miller's Parisian meanderings and coffeeshop squabble.<br /><br />As with every comparative work to Ulysses the result falls somewhere in its broad shadow. Still, I'm peachy keen to read more Croatian lit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5604448648593542373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Nick Borkowski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick Borkowski" href="/user/show/19091781-nick-borkowski">Nick Borkowski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1187264088&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1187264088" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16492024504516283848">Well worth the read. It took some time for me to finish Cyclops. It is certainly less ambitious than Ulysses in it's experimentation with form, but perhaps more focused as a criticism of world war. Melkior is less appealing than Poldy. I felt less empathy for him and he was not as human for me as Mr. Bloom. Cyclops remains an engaging read. I didn't catch the Croatian references and look forward to some day rereading to gain more from the novel. Some colorful images remain: a crocodile as war in</span>
  <span id="freeText16492024504516283848" style="display:none">Well worth the read. It took some time for me to finish Cyclops. It is certainly less ambitious than Ulysses in it's experimentation with form, but perhaps more focused as a criticism of world war. Melkior is less appealing than Poldy. I felt less empathy for him and he was not as human for me as Mr. Bloom. Cyclops remains an engaging read. I didn't catch the Croatian references and look forward to some day rereading to gain more from the novel. Some colorful images remain: a crocodile as war instrument; the suicide of a man peeing on an electric wire; the X of the mens and women's restrooms; and a scale weight in an avenue. Sadly no charmed potato in a pocket.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16492024504516283848" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oJkE2uiVB5Bz8fw+dBc4OEV80LM4UG4zvdkeQShnJBouuU6G4mgfPIdG8LEDMyYEaiY8ONDd/Nme+h8BX7DNhA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gyifXTCxXtNBXfaXxEOH9rTXT17qUEVWfbmPdPiswdsNCNUBOkxGf7Xq+hizZ5nKm42j1QLd17xemo40j3soRQ==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_11553583'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11553583-prokleta-avlija?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Prokleta avlija<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2147.Ivo_Andri_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivo Andrić<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.30 avg rating &mdash; 7,170 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1954\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer15729181431196616637\">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)\n\nThe novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15729181431196616637\" style=\"display:none\">Prokleta avlija/The Damned Yard (Description from Ivo Andrić Foundation website)\n\nThe novel is written in 1954. Ćamil, a wealthy young man of Smyrna living in the last years of the Ottoman Empire, is fascinated by the story of Džem, ill-fated brother of the Sultan Bajazet, who ruled Turkey in the fifteenth century. Ćamil, in his isolation, comes to believe that he is Džem, and that he shares his evil destiny: he is born to be a victim of the State. Because of his stories about Džem’s ambitions to overthrow his brother, Ćamil is arrested under suspicion of plotting against the Sultan. He is taken to a prison in Istanbul, where he tells his story, to Petar, a monk.\n\nOut of these exotic materials, Andrić has constructed a book of great clarity, brevity and interest. No doubt it will be read by some as a political parable about the tyranny of the State, but also as a quite simply story about ill-fortune and human misunderstanding, fear and ignorance. Džem and Ćamil are doomed – and the certainty of their persecution is sometimes relieved, sometimes intensified by the stupidity and fright of the people who cross their ill-starred lives.\n\nConstruction takes up most of the book’s space: the central story of Džem as related by Ćamil lasts only a chapter or two. For the rest of the time the reader strips layer off layer, as one narrator passes him on the next. There is an interesting passage that helps to explain this method, at the moment when Ćamil starts narrating Džem’s story in the first person. “I” is a word, we are told, which fixes the position of the speaker in such a way that the exercise of will is no longer possible, and the speaker strength is exceeded – strength, presumably, to break out of the identification that all his past actions and thoughts force upon him when he uses the word. “I” is both a confession and an imprisonment. The fact that the novel passes the reader on from one narrator to the next rather suggests that the author is taking constant evasive action, lest he betray himself or his reader into the kind of “personal confession” which seals the fate of Ćamil. What exactly this game of form flirting with meaning signifies, must be left to the individual reader.\n\nThe movement is centripetal, towards Džem’s story, and then disperses. Details within the story are made to mimic this form. Thus when Peter receives the message telling him of his impending release:\n\n“Two younger prisoners...were chasing around using him as the centerpoint of ever narrowing circles. Annoyed, he tried to break away from these exuberant youths when one of them brushed against him and he felt a folded scrap of paper thrust into his hand. The youths continued their chase but now in widening circles...”\n\nThe reader is led on just such a chase in the course of the novel. The effect of this is to make the plot seem more like a poetic image than an ordinary plot: capable, therefore, of as many meanings as are the images of an allusive poem. Yet the language is simple and direct, not at all “poetic”. The characters are remarkable alive, even in conversation. Karađoz, the governor of the goal, is a spidery authoritarian, who loves to torment the charges he loves. The prisoners “complained about the way one complains about one’s life and curses one’s destiny...it would have been hard for them to imagine life without him”.\n\n“The Devil’s Yard” is justified, as all symbolic and figurative novels must be, by the extent to which it touches the emotions. It is extremely moving. Fear, horror, despair, amusement at times – all these indicate that the threat of the meaning has been recognized.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15729181431196616637\" href=\"#\" <script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_4576186'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4576186-pri-e-iz-davnine?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Priče iz davnine<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/2975270.Ivana_Brli_Ma_urani_?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Ivana Brlić-Mažuranić<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.19 avg rating &mdash; 2,146 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1916\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13793150142894944695\">Priče iz davnine, glasovita zbirka od osam pripovijetki-bajki Ivane Brlić-Mažuranić, u kojima je autorica ostvarila svoj književni vrhunac. Zbirka je objavljena 1916. u izdanju Matice hrvatske, a drugo izdanje doživljava 1926.\n\nRadnju pokreću likovi <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13793150142894944695\" style=\"display:none\">Priče iz davnine, glasovita zbirka od osam pripovijetki-bajki Ivane Brlić-Mažuranić, u kojima je autorica ostvarila svoj književni vrhunac. Zbirka je objavljena 1916. u izdanju Matice hrvatske, a drugo izdanje doživljava 1926.\n\nRadnju pokreću likovi iz pretkršćanske, slavenske mitologije, koje je Brlić-Mažuranić, inače i dvostruka kandidatkinja za Nobelovu nagradu za književnost, stilizirala davši im određene osobine. Posluživši se oblikom bajke i fantastičnim elementima, stvorila je autonomni svijet davnine u kojem se mitski svijet spaja sa secesijski stiliziranom slikom vremena, prostora, likova i događaja. Prvo izdanje je imalo šest priča, a u drugom izdanju su dodane još dvije.\n\nCroatian Tales of Long Ago (Croatian: Priče iz davnine), is a short story collection written by the acclaimed children\'s author Ivana Brlić-Mažuranić (sometimes spelled as &quot;Ivana Berlić-Mažuranić&quot; in English), originally published in 1916 in Zagreb by the Matica hrvatska publishing house. The collection is considered her masterpiece and it features a series of newly written fairy tales heavily inspired by motifs taken from ancient Slavic mythology of pre-Christian Croatia.\n\nCroatian Tales of Long Ago are seen as one of the most typical examples of her writing style which has been compared by literary critics to Hans Christian Andersen and J. R. R. Tolkien due to the way it combines original fantasy plots with folk mythology. It recounts the story of Sheikh Nuruddin, a dervish residing in an Islamic monastery in Sarajevo in the eighteenth century during the Ottoman Turk hegemony over the Balkans. When his brother is arrested, he must descend into the Kafkaesque world of the Ottoman authorities in his search to discover what happened to him. He narrates his story in the form of an elaborate suicide note, regularly misquoting the Koran. In time, he begins to question his relations with society as a whole and, eventually, his life choices in general. Evo kako ga sažima Igor Mrduljaš:\n&quot;Na sastanku Mjesnog aktiva Narodnog fronta u selu Mrduša Donja raspravlja se o \'kulturno-prosvjetnoj aktivnosti\'. Mjesni učitelj Škunca kušat će e ukloniti obvezi režiranja predstave, no i njega će Bukara upozoriti da \'mora izvršavati obaveze koje narod prid tebeka postavlja\'. Zadrugar Šimurina gledao je u Zagrebu izvedbu Hamleta i potanko je prepričava:\'naprednog, socijalistički orijentiranog kralja\' ubio je njegov brat,\'neprijatelj naroda, ljuta reakcija\', koji je potom\'priokrenuo\' kraljicu-udovu. Kraljev sin \'Amlet\' koji od \'puste žalosti đisa po kući gorika dolika\' nagovori neku \'kulturno-umjetničku sekciju\' da napravi \'demonstraciju\' pred novim kraljem i \'ukažu mu na njegove slabosti\' na što se ovaj \'pripa, pobiga i sakrija se nigdi...\' Potom \'Amlet\' ubije \'Omelijiog ćaću\', a nju pokuša otjerati \'fratrima\'. \'Napredna cura\' ga ne posluša i radije \'skoči u niku lokvetinu i udavi se\'. Usred priče upadne Škoko, sin zadružnog blagajnika i napadne Bukaru da mu je dao zatvoriti oca. Bukara arogantno odbije objedu tvrdeći kako mu je otac pronevjerio zadružni novac. Po odlasku mladog Škoke koji je zaprijetio osvetom, seljaci odlučuju da postave Hamleta. Učitelj je očajan, ali nema izlaza. Njegova podjela uloga pokazat će se dalekovidnom: Bukara igra Klaudija, predsjednik Mjesnog aktiva Narodnog fornta Puljo uvježbava Polonija, pustopašna krčmarica Majkača Gertrudu, gnjevni Škoko Hamleta, a Puljina kći Anđa Ofeliju. Miroslav Krieia schreef het boek, dat uit een cyclus van zeven novellen bestaat, onmiddellijk na de Eerste Wereldoorlog. Het is een synthese van de Kroatische geschiedenis, en geeft een heldere kijk op Centraal-Europa en op de normen en waarden uit de Europese beschaving die tijdens de oorlog sneuvelden. Maar het is ook een humanistisch pleidooi voor de rechten van het vertrapte individu. Tegelijkertijd fungeert het als een scherpzinnige visie op de menselijke existentie in het algemeen: het boek overschrijdt dan ook in belangrijke mate het tijdperk dat erin beschreven staat.\n\nNiets in De Kroatische god Mars is definitief bepaald: achter serieuze gedachten bevindt zich ironie, achter tragiek schuilt humor, achter het sentimentele het groteske. Krie2a drukt zich uit in een ruwe, alledaagse taal, die wordt afgewisseld met milde, subtiele, fantasierijke passages. Zijn stijl is hard, beknopt, direct, en dan weer gevoelig, symbolisch. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5797982299826410536">Ranko Marinković (22 February 1913 – 28 January 2001) was a Croatian novelist and dramatist.<br/><br/>Born in Komiža on the island of Vis (then a part of Austria-Hungary), Marinković's childhood was marked by World War I. He later earned a degree in philosophy from the University of Zagreb. In the 1930s, he began to make his name in Zagreb literary circles with his plays and stories.<br/><br/>His career was interrupt</span>
  <span id="freeText5797982299826410536" style="display:none">Ranko Marinković (22 February 1913 – 28 January 2001) was a Croatian novelist and dramatist.<br /><br />Born in Komiža on the island of Vis (then a part of Austria-Hungary), Marinković's childhood was marked by World War I. He later earned a degree in philosophy from the University of Zagreb. In the 1930s, he began to make his name in Zagreb literary circles with his plays and stories.<br /><br />His career was interrupted briefly during World War II. When his native island was occupied by fascist Italy, he was arrested in Split and interned on the Italian mainland. After the capitulation of Italy, Marinković went to Bari, and then to the El Shatt refugee camp where he made contacts with Tito's Partisans. After the war, he spent time working in the theatre.<br /><br />His best known works are Glorija (1955), a play in which he criticised the Catholic Church, and Kiklop (1965), a semi-autobiographical novel in which he described the gloomy atmosphere among Zagreb intellectuals before the Axis invasion of Yugoslavia. Kiklop later was adapted into a 1982 movie directed by Antun Vrdoljak.<br /><br />In the last years of his life Marinković embraced the political views of Franjo Tuđman, and became a member of the Croatian Democratic Union.<br /><br />He died in Zagreb.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5797982299826410536" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
